<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">David Bowie</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

<p><b>David Robert Jones</b> (8 January 1947 – 10 January 2016), known professionally as <b>David Bowie</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-saybowe_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-saybowe-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> was an English singer, songwriter and actor. He was a leading figure in the music industry and is often considered to be one of the most influential musicians of the 20th century, acclaimed by critics and fellow musicians, particularly for his innovative work during the 1970s. His career was marked by reinvention and visual presentation, with his music and stagecraft having a significant impact on <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a>. During his lifetime, his record sales, estimated at 140 million albums worldwide, made him one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_music_artists" title="List of best-selling music artists">world's best-selling music artists</a>. In the UK, he was awarded ten platinum album certifications, eleven gold and eight silver, releasing <a href="/wiki/List_of_artists_by_number_of_UK_Albums_Chart_number_ones" title="List of artists by number of UK Albums Chart number ones">eleven number-one albums</a>. In the US, he received five platinum and nine gold certifications. He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> in 1996.
</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Brixton" title="Brixton">Brixton</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_London" title="South London">South London</a>, Bowie developed an interest in music as a child, eventually studying art, music, and design before embarking on a professional career as a musician in 1963. "<a href="/wiki/Space_Oddity" title="Space Oddity">Space Oddity</a>" became his first top-five entry on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">UK Singles Chart</a> after its release in July 1969. After a period of experimentation, he re-emerged in 1972 during the <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">glam rock</a> era with his flamboyant and androgynous <a href="/wiki/Alter_ego" title="Alter ego">alter ego</a> Ziggy Stardust. The character was spearheaded by the success of his single "<a href="/wiki/Starman_(song)" title="Starman (song)">Starman</a>" and album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_Ziggy_Stardust_and_the_Spiders_from_Mars" title="The Rise and Fall of Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars">The Rise and Fall of Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars</a></i>, which won him widespread popularity. In 1975, Bowie's style shifted radically towards a sound he characterised as "<a href="/wiki/Plastic_soul" title="Plastic soul">plastic soul</a>", initially alienating many of his UK devotees but garnering him his first major US crossover success with the number-one single "<a href="/wiki/Fame_(David_Bowie_song)" title="Fame (David Bowie song)">Fame</a>" and the album <i><a href="/wiki/Young_Americans" title="Young Americans">Young Americans</a></i>. In 1976, Bowie starred in the cult film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Fell_to_Earth" title="The Man Who Fell to Earth">The Man Who Fell to Earth</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Roeg" title="Nicolas Roeg">Nicolas Roeg</a>, and released <i><a href="/wiki/Station_to_Station" title="Station to Station">Station to Station</a></i>. The following year, he further confounded musical expectations with the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic</a>-inflected album <i><a href="/wiki/Low_(David_Bowie_album)" title="Low (David Bowie album)">Low</a></i> (1977), the first of three collaborations with <a href="/wiki/Brian_Eno" title="Brian Eno">Brian Eno</a> that would come to be known as the "<a href="/wiki/Berlin_Trilogy" title="Berlin Trilogy">Berlin Trilogy</a>". <i><a href="/wiki/%22Heroes%22_(David_Bowie_album)" title="&quot;Heroes&quot; (David Bowie album)">"Heroes"</a></i> (1977) and <i><a href="/wiki/Lodger_(album)" title="Lodger (album)">Lodger</a></i> (1979) followed; each album reached the UK top five and received lasting critical praise.
</p><p>After uneven commercial success in the late 1970s, Bowie had UK number ones with the 1980 single "<a href="/wiki/Ashes_to_Ashes_(David_Bowie_song)" title="Ashes to Ashes (David Bowie song)">Ashes to Ashes</a>", its parent album <i><a href="/wiki/Scary_Monsters_(and_Super_Creeps)" title="Scary Monsters (and Super Creeps)">Scary Monsters (and Super Creeps)</a></i>, and "<a href="/wiki/Under_Pressure" title="Under Pressure">Under Pressure</a>", a 1981 collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a>. He then reached his commercial peak in 1983 with <i><a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Dance_(David_Bowie_album)" title="Let&#39;s Dance (David Bowie album)">Let's Dance</a></i>, with its <a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Dance_(David_Bowie_song)" title="Let&#39;s Dance (David Bowie song)">title track</a> topping both UK and US charts. Throughout the 1990s and 2000s, Bowie continued to experiment with musical styles, including <a href="/wiki/Industrial_music" title="Industrial music">industrial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drum_and_bass" title="Drum and bass">jungle</a>. He also continued acting; his roles included Major Jack Celliers in <i><a href="/wiki/Merry_Christmas,_Mr._Lawrence" title="Merry Christmas, Mr. Lawrence">Merry Christmas, Mr. Lawrence</a></i> (1983), <a href="/wiki/Jareth" title="Jareth">Jareth</a> the Goblin King in <i><a href="/wiki/Labyrinth_(film)" title="Labyrinth (film)">Labyrinth</a></i> (1986), <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Temptation_of_Christ_(film)" title="The Last Temptation of Christ (film)">The Last Temptation of Christ</a></i> (1988), and <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prestige_(film)" title="The Prestige (film)">The Prestige</a></i> (2006), among other film and television appearances and cameos. He stopped concert touring after 2004 and his last live performance was at a charity event in 2006. In 2013, Bowie returned from a decade-long recording hiatus with the release of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Next_Day" title="The Next Day">The Next Day</a>.</i> He remained musically active until he <a href="/wiki/Death_of_David_Bowie" title="Death of David Bowie">died of liver cancer</a> two days after the release of his final album, <i><a href="/wiki/Blackstar_(album)" title="Blackstar (album)">Blackstar</a></i> (2016).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bowie was born David Robert Jones on 8 January 1947 in <a href="/wiki/Brixton" title="Brixton">Brixton</a>, London. His mother, Margaret Mary "Peggy" (née Burns; 1913–2001),<sup id="cite_ref-bowiewonderworld.com_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowiewonderworld.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> was born at <a href="/wiki/Shorncliffe_Army_Camp" title="Shorncliffe Army Camp">Shorncliffe Army Camp</a> near <a href="/wiki/Cheriton,_Kent" title="Cheriton, Kent">Cheriton</a>, Kent.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Her paternal grandparents were Irish immigrants who had settled in Manchester.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> She worked as a waitress at a cinema in <a href="/wiki/Royal_Tunbridge_Wells" title="Royal Tunbridge Wells">Royal Tunbridge Wells</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His father, Haywood Stenton "John" Jones (1912–1969),<sup id="cite_ref-bowiewonderworld.com_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowiewonderworld.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> was from <a href="/wiki/Doncaster" title="Doncaster">Doncaster</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and worked as a promotions officer for the children's charity <a href="/wiki/Barnardo%27s" title="Barnardo&#39;s">Barnardo's</a>. The family lived at 40 Stansfield Road, on the boundary between Brixton and <a href="/wiki/Stockwell" title="Stockwell">Stockwell</a> in the south London borough of Lambeth. Bowie attended <a href="/wiki/Stockwell_Primary_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Stockwell Primary School">Stockwell Infants School</a> until he was six years old, acquiring a reputation as a gifted and single-minded child—and a defiant brawler.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1953, Bowie moved with his family to <a href="/wiki/Bromley" title="Bromley">Bromley</a>. Two years later, he started attending Burnt Ash Junior School. His voice was considered "adequate" by the school choir, and he demonstrated above-average abilities in playing the <a href="/wiki/Recorder_(musical_instrument)" title="Recorder (musical instrument)">recorder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford18-19_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford18-19-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> At the age of nine, his dancing during the newly-introduced music and movement classes was strikingly imaginative: teachers called his interpretations "vividly artistic" and his poise "astonishing" for a child.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford18-19_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford18-19-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The same year, his interest in music was further stimulated when his father brought home a collection of American <a href="/wiki/Gramophone_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramophone record">45s</a> by artists including <a href="/wiki/The_Teenagers" title="The Teenagers">the Teenagers</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Platters" title="The Platters">the Platters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fats_Domino" title="Fats Domino">Fats Domino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Little_Richard" title="Little Richard">Little Richard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sandford19-20_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford19-20-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Upon listening to Little Richard's song "<a href="/wiki/Tutti_Frutti_(song)" title="Tutti Frutti (song)">Tutti Frutti</a>", Bowie would later say that he had "heard God".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bowie was first impressed with Presley when he saw his cousin dance to "<a href="/wiki/Hound_Dog_(song)" title="Hound Dog (song)">Hound Dog</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford19-20_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford19-20-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the following year, he had taken up the <a href="/wiki/Ukulele" title="Ukulele">ukulele</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea-chest_bass" class="mw-redirect" title="Tea-chest bass">tea-chest bass</a>, begun to participate in <a href="/wiki/Skiffle" title="Skiffle">skiffle</a> sessions with friends, and had started to play the piano; meanwhile, his stage presentation of numbers by both Presley and <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a>—complete with gyrations in tribute to the original artists—to his local <a href="/wiki/Cub_Scout" title="Cub Scout">Wolf Cub</a> group was described as "mesmerizing&#160;... like someone from another planet".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford19-20_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford19-20-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> After taking his <a href="/wiki/Eleven-plus" title="Eleven-plus">eleven-plus</a> exam at the conclusion of his Burnt Ash Junior education, Bowie went to <a href="/wiki/Ravens_Wood_School" title="Ravens Wood School">Bromley Technical High School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bromleytech_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bromleytech-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was an unusual technical school, as biographer Christopher Sandford wrote:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Despite its status it was, by the time David arrived in 1958, as rich in arcane ritual as any [English] <a href="/wiki/Independent_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Independent school (United Kingdom)">public school</a>. There were <a href="/wiki/House_system" title="House system">houses</a> named after eighteenth-century statesmen like <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt,_1st_Earl_of_Chatham" title="William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham">Pitt</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Wilberforce" title="William Wilberforce">Wilberforce</a>. There was a uniform, and an elaborate system of rewards and punishments. There was also an accent on languages, science and particularly design, where a collegiate atmosphere flourished under the tutorship of Owen Frampton. In David's account, Frampton led through force of personality, not intellect; his colleagues at Bromley Tech were famous for neither, and yielded the school's most gifted pupils to the arts, a regime so liberal that Frampton actively encouraged his own son, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Frampton" title="Peter Frampton">Peter</a>, to pursue a musical career with David, a partnership briefly intact thirty years later.<sup id="cite_ref-bromleytech_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bromleytech-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Bowie studied art, music, and design, including layout and typesetting. After his half-brother Terry Burns introduced him to modern jazz, his enthusiasm for players like <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mingus" title="Charles Mingus">Charles Mingus</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Coltrane" title="John Coltrane">John Coltrane</a> led his mother to give him a <a href="/wiki/Grafton_saxophone" title="Grafton saxophone">Grafton saxophone</a> in 1961. He was soon receiving lessons from <a href="/wiki/Baritone_saxophone" title="Baritone saxophone">baritone saxophonist</a> <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_Ross" title="Ronnie Ross">Ronnie Ross</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He received a serious injury at school in 1962 when his friend <a href="/wiki/George_Underwood_(artist)" title="George Underwood (artist)">George Underwood</a> punched him in the left eye during a fight over a girl. After a series of operations during a four-month hospitalisation,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> his doctors determined that the damage could not be fully repaired and Bowie was left with faulty <a href="/wiki/Depth_perception" title="Depth perception">depth perception</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Anisocoria" title="Anisocoria">permanently dilated pupil</a>, which gave a false impression of a <a href="/wiki/Heterochromia_iridum" title="Heterochromia iridum">change in the iris' colour</a>; the eye later became one of Bowie's most recognisable features.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Despite their altercation, Bowie remained on good terms with Underwood, who went on to create the artwork for Bowie's early albums.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="1962.E2.80.931967:_Early_career_to_debut_album"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1962–1967:_Early_career_to_debut_album">1962–1967: Early career to debut album</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1962–1967: Early career to debut album">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_(1967).png" class="image"><img alt="Bowie looking to the camera" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/David_Bowie_%281967%29.png/170px-David_Bowie_%281967%29.png" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/David_Bowie_%281967%29.png/255px-David_Bowie_%281967%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/David_Bowie_%281967%29.png/340px-David_Bowie_%281967%29.png 2x" data-file-width="452" data-file-height="547" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_(1967).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A trade ad photo of Bowie in 1967</div></div></div>
<p>In 1962, Bowie formed his first band at the age of 15, named the Konrads. Playing guitar-based <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a> at local youth gatherings and weddings, the Konrads had a varying line-up of between four and eight members, Underwood among them.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> When Bowie left the technical school the following year, he informed his parents of his intention to become a pop star. His mother arranged his employment as an electrician's mate. Frustrated by his bandmates' limited aspirations, Bowie left the Konrads and joined another band, the King Bees. He wrote to the newly successful washing-machine entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/John_Bloom_(businessman)" title="John Bloom (businessman)">John Bloom</a> inviting him to "do for us what <a href="/wiki/Brian_Epstein" title="Brian Epstein">Brian Epstein</a> has done for <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>—and make another million." Bloom did not respond to the offer, but his referral to <a href="/wiki/Dick_James" title="Dick James">Dick James</a>'s partner Leslie Conn led to Bowie's first personal management contract.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford29-30_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford29-30-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Conn quickly began to promote Bowie. The singer's debut single, "<a href="/wiki/Liza_Jane_(David_Bowie_song)" title="Liza Jane (David Bowie song)">Liza Jane</a>", credited to Davie Jones with the King Bees, was not commercially successful. Dissatisfied with the King Bees and their repertoire of <a href="/wiki/Howlin%27_Wolf" title="Howlin&#39; Wolf">Howlin' Wolf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Dixon" title="Willie Dixon">Willie Dixon</a> covers, Bowie quit the band less than a month later to join the Mannish Boys, another blues outfit, who incorporated folk and soul—"I used to dream of being their <a href="/wiki/Mick_Jagger" title="Mick Jagger">Mick Jagger</a>", Bowie was to recall.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford29-30_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford29-30-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Their cover of <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Bland" title="Bobby Bland">Bobby Bland</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/I_Pity_the_Fool" title="I Pity the Fool">I Pity the Fool</a>" was no more successful than "Liza Jane", and Bowie soon moved on again to join the Lower Third, a blues trio strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>. "<a href="/wiki/You%27ve_Got_a_Habit_of_Leaving" title="You&#39;ve Got a Habit of Leaving">You've Got a Habit of Leaving</a>" fared no better, signalling the end of Conn's contract. Declaring that he would exit the pop music world "to study mime at <a href="/wiki/Sadler%27s_Wells_Theatre" title="Sadler&#39;s Wells Theatre">Sadler's Wells</a>", Bowie nevertheless remained with the Lower Third. His new manager, Ralph Horton, later instrumental in his transition to solo artist, soon witnessed Bowie's move to yet another group, the Buzz, yielding the singer's fifth unsuccessful single release, "<a href="/wiki/Do_Anything_You_Say" title="Do Anything You Say">Do Anything You Say</a>". While with the Buzz, Bowie also joined the <a href="/wiki/The_Riot_Squad" title="The Riot Squad">Riot Squad</a>; their recordings, which included one of Bowie's original songs and material by <a href="/wiki/The_Velvet_Underground" title="The Velvet Underground">The Velvet Underground</a>, went unreleased. Ken Pitt, introduced by Horton, took over as Bowie's manager.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dissatisfied with his stage name as Davy (and Davie) Jones, which in the mid-1960s invited confusion with <a href="/wiki/Davy_Jones_(musician)" title="Davy Jones (musician)">Davy Jones</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Monkees" title="The Monkees">The Monkees</a>, Bowie renamed himself after the 19th-century <a href="/wiki/American_pioneer" title="American pioneer">American pioneer</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Bowie" title="James Bowie">James Bowie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bowie_knife" title="Bowie knife">the knife he had popularised</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> His April 1967 solo single, "<a href="/wiki/The_Laughing_Gnome" title="The Laughing Gnome">The Laughing Gnome</a>", using speeded-up thus high-pitched vocals, failed to chart. Released six weeks later, his album debut, <i><a href="/wiki/David_Bowie_(1967_album)" title="David Bowie (1967 album)">David Bowie</a></i>, an amalgam of pop, <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">music hall</a>, met the same fate. It was his last release for two years.<sup id="cite_ref-albumdebut_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-albumdebut-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1968.E2.80.931971:_Space_Oddity_to_Hunky_Dory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1968–1971:_Space_Oddity_to_Hunky_Dory">1968–1971: <i>Space Oddity</i> to <i>Hunky Dory</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1968–1971: Space Oddity to Hunky Dory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="Hermione_Farthingale"></span>
</p><p>Bowie met dancer <a href="/wiki/Lindsay_Kemp" title="Lindsay Kemp">Lindsay Kemp</a> in 1967 and enrolled in his dance class at the London Dance Centre.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> He commented in 1972 that meeting Kemp was when his interest in image "really blossomed".<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> "He lived on his emotions, he was a wonderful influence. His day-to-day life was the most theatrical thing I had ever seen, ever. It was everything I thought Bohemia probably was. I joined the circus."<sup id="cite_ref-buckley41-42_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckley41-42-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Studying the dramatic arts under Kemp, from <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> theatre and <a href="/wiki/Mime_artist" title="Mime artist">mime</a> to <a href="/wiki/Commedia_dell%27arte" title="Commedia dell&#39;arte">commedia dell'arte</a>, Bowie became immersed in the creation of personae to present to the world. Satirising life in a British prison, meanwhile, the Bowie-penned "Over the Wall We Go" became a 1967 single for <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nicholas" title="Paul Nicholas">Oscar</a>; another Bowie composition, "Silly Boy Blue", was released by <a href="/wiki/Billy_Fury" title="Billy Fury">Billy Fury</a> the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In January 1968, Kemp choreographed a dance scene for a <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> play, <i>The Pistol Shot</i>, in the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_625" title="Theatre 625">Theatre 625</a> series, and used Bowie with a dancer, Hermione Farthingale;<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> the pair began dating, and moved into a London flat together. Playing acoustic guitar, Farthingale formed a group with Bowie and guitarist John Hutchinson; between September 1968 and early 1969 the trio gave a small number of concerts combining folk, <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">Merseybeat</a>, poetry, and mime.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Bowie and Farthingale broke up in early 1969 when she went to Norway to take part in a film, <i><a href="/wiki/Song_of_Norway_(film)" title="Song of Norway (film)">Song of Norway</a></i>;<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> this affected him, and several songs, such as "<a href="/wiki/Letter_to_Hermione" class="mw-redirect" title="Letter to Hermione">Letter to Hermione</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars_(song)" title="Life on Mars (song)">Life on Mars?</a>" reference her,<sup id="cite_ref-HeatleyHopkinson2014_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HeatleyHopkinson2014-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and for the video accompanying "<a href="/wiki/Where_Are_We_Now%3F" title="Where Are We Now?">Where Are We Now?</a>", he wore a T-shirt with the words "m/s Song of Norway".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> They were last together in January 1969 for the filming of <i><a href="/wiki/Love_You_till_Tuesday_(film)" title="Love You till Tuesday (film)">Love You till Tuesday</a></i>, a 30-minute film that was not released until 1984: intended as a promotional vehicle, it featured performances from Bowie's repertoire, including "<a href="/wiki/Space_Oddity" title="Space Oddity">Space Oddity</a>", which had not been released when the film was made.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford49-50_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford49-50-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the break-up with Farthingale, Bowie moved in with Mary Finnigan as her lodger.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford53_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford53-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> During this period he appeared in a <a href="/wiki/Lyons_Maid" title="Lyons Maid">Lyons Maid</a> ice cream commercial, and was rejected for another by <a href="/wiki/Kit_Kat" title="Kit Kat">Kit Kat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford49-50_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford49-50-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In February and March 1969, he undertook a short tour with <a href="/wiki/Marc_Bolan" title="Marc Bolan">Marc Bolan</a>'s duo <a href="/wiki/T._Rex_(band)" title="T. Rex (band)">Tyrannosaurus Rex</a>, as third on the bill, performing a mime act.<sup id="cite_ref-Paytress2009_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paytress2009-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> On 11 July 1969, "Space Oddity" was released five days ahead of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> launch, and reached the top five in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford49-50_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford49-50-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Continuing the divergence from rock and roll and blues begun by his work with Farthingale, Bowie joined forces with Finnigan, Christina Ostrom and Barrie Jackson to run a folk club on Sunday nights at the Three Tuns pub in <a href="/wiki/Beckenham" title="Beckenham">Beckenham</a> High Street.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford53_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford53-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Arts_Lab" title="Arts Lab">Arts Lab</a> movement, this developed into the <a href="/wiki/Beckenham_Arts_Lab" class="mw-redirect" title="Beckenham Arts Lab">Beckenham Arts Lab</a>, and became extremely popular. The Arts Lab hosted a free festival in a local park, the subject of his song "<a href="/wiki/Memory_of_a_Free_Festival" title="Memory of a Free Festival">Memory of a Free Festival</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Bowie's second album followed in November; originally issued in the UK as <i><a href="/wiki/David_Bowie_(1969_album)" title="David Bowie (1969 album)">David Bowie</a></i>, it caused some confusion with its predecessor of the same name, and the early US release was instead titled <i>Man of Words/Man of Music</i>; it was reissued internationally in 1972 by <a href="/wiki/RCA_Records" title="RCA Records">RCA Records</a> as <i>Space Oddity</i>. Featuring philosophical post-<a href="/wiki/Hippie" title="Hippie">hippie</a> lyrics on peace, love, and morality, its acoustic folk rock occasionally fortified by harder rock, the album was not a commercial success at the time of its release.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bowie met <a href="/wiki/Angie_Bowie" title="Angie Bowie">Angela Barnett</a> in April 1969. They married within a year. Her impact on him was immediate, and her involvement in his career far-reaching, leaving manager Ken Pitt with limited influence which he found frustrating.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford54-60_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford54-60-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Having established himself as a solo artist with "Space Oddity", Bowie began to sense a lacking: "a full-time band for gigs and recording—people he could relate to personally".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford62-63_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford62-63-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The shortcoming was underlined by his artistic rivalry with <a href="/wiki/Marc_Bolan" title="Marc Bolan">Marc Bolan</a>, who was at the time acting as his session guitarist.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford62-63_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford62-63-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> A band was duly assembled. John Cambridge, a drummer Bowie met at the Arts Lab, was joined by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Visconti" title="Tony Visconti">Tony Visconti</a> on bass and <a href="/wiki/Mick_Ronson" title="Mick Ronson">Mick Ronson</a> on electric guitar. Known as <a href="/wiki/The_Hype_(David_Bowie_band)" title="The Hype (David Bowie band)">the Hype</a>, the bandmates created characters for themselves and wore elaborate costumes that prefigured the glam style of the Spiders from Mars. After a disastrous opening gig at the <a href="/wiki/Roundhouse_(venue)" title="Roundhouse (venue)">London Roundhouse</a>, they reverted to a configuration presenting Bowie as a solo artist.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford62-63_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford62-63-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Their initial studio work was marred by a heated disagreement between Bowie and Cambridge over the latter's drumming style. Matters came to a head when an enraged Bowie accused the drummer of the disturbance, exclaiming "You're fucking up my album." Cambridge summarily quit and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Mick_Woodmansey" title="Mick Woodmansey">Mick Woodmansey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford67_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford67-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Not long after, the singer fired his manager and replaced him with <a href="/wiki/Tony_Defries" title="Tony Defries">Tony Defries</a>. This resulted in years of litigation that concluded with Bowie having to pay Pitt compensation.<sup id="cite_ref-sandford67_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford67-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The studio sessions continued and resulted in Bowie's third album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Sold_the_World_(album)" title="The Man Who Sold the World (album)">The Man Who Sold the World</a></i> (1970), which contained references to <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>, paranoia, and delusion.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Characterised by the heavy rock sound of his new <a href="/wiki/Backup_band" title="Backup band">backing band</a>, it was a marked departure from the acoustic guitar and folk rock style established by <i>Space Oddity</i>. To promote it in the US, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Records" title="Mercury Records">Mercury Records</a> financed a coast-to-coast publicity tour across America in which Bowie, between January and February 1971, was interviewed by radio stations and the media. Exploiting his <a href="/wiki/Androgyny" title="Androgyny">androgynous</a> appearance, the original cover of the UK version unveiled two months later depicted the singer wearing a dress: taking the garment with him, he wore it during interviews—to the approval of critics, including <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s <a href="/wiki/John_Mendelsohn_(musician)" title="John Mendelsohn (musician)">John Mendelsohn</a> who described him as "ravishing, almost disconcertingly reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Lauren_Bacall" title="Lauren Bacall">Lauren Bacall</a>"&#160; – and in the street, to mixed reaction including laughter and, in the case of one male pedestrian, producing a gun and telling Bowie to "kiss my ass".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford73-74_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford73-74-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the tour, Bowie's observation of two seminal American <a href="/wiki/Proto-punk" title="Proto-punk">proto-punk</a> artists led him to develop a concept that eventually found form in the Ziggy Stardust character: a melding of the persona of <a href="/wiki/Iggy_Pop" title="Iggy Pop">Iggy Pop</a> with the music of <a href="/wiki/Lou_Reed" title="Lou Reed">Lou Reed</a>, producing "the ultimate pop idol".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford73-74_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford73-74-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> A girlfriend recalled his "scrawling notes on a cocktail napkin about a crazy rock star named Iggy or Ziggy", and on his return to England he declared his intention to create a character "who looks like he's landed from Mars".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford73-74_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford73-74-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The "Stardust" surname was a tribute to the "<a href="/wiki/Legendary_Stardust_Cowboy" title="Legendary Stardust Cowboy">Legendary Stardust Cowboy</a>", whose record he was given during the tour. Bowie would later cover "I Took a Trip on a Gemini Space Ship" on 2002's <i><a href="/wiki/Heathen_(David_Bowie_album)" title="Heathen (David Bowie album)">Heathen</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2009177_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2009177-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Hunky_Dory" title="Hunky Dory">Hunky Dory</a></i> (1971) found Visconti, Bowie's producer and bassist, supplanted in both roles by <a href="/wiki/Ken_Scott" title="Ken Scott">Ken Scott</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Bolder" title="Trevor Bolder">Trevor Bolder</a> respectively. The album saw the partial return of the fey pop singer of "Space Oddity", with light fare such as "<a href="/wiki/Kooks_(song)" title="Kooks (song)">Kooks</a>", a song written for his son, <a href="/wiki/Duncan_Jones" title="Duncan Jones">Duncan Zowie Haywood Jones</a>, born on 30 May.<sup id="cite_ref-buckley95-99_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckley95-99-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> (His parents chose "his kooky name"—he was known as Zowie for the next 12 years—after the Greek word <i>zoe</i>, life.)<sup id="cite_ref-sandford80_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford80-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere, the album explored more serious subjects, and found Bowie paying unusually direct homage to his influences with "<a href="/wiki/Song_for_Bob_Dylan" title="Song for Bob Dylan">Song for Bob Dylan</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol_(song)" title="Andy Warhol (song)">Andy Warhol</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/Queen_Bitch" title="Queen Bitch">Queen Bitch</a>", a <a href="/wiki/The_Velvet_Underground" title="The Velvet Underground">Velvet Underground</a> pastiche. It was not a significant commercial success at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1972.E2.80.931974:_Ziggy_Stardust"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1972–1974:_Ziggy_Stardust">1972–1974: <i>Ziggy Stardust</i><span id="Ziggy_Stardust"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1972–1974: Ziggy Stardust">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David-Bowie_Early.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/David-Bowie_Early.jpg/170px-David-Bowie_Early.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/David-Bowie_Early.jpg/255px-David-Bowie_Early.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/David-Bowie_Early.jpg 2x" data-file-width="302" data-file-height="422" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David-Bowie_Early.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie during the <a href="/wiki/Ziggy_Stardust_Tour" title="Ziggy Stardust Tour">Ziggy Stardust Tour</a> from 1972–73</div></div></div>
<p>Dressed in a striking costume, his hair dyed reddish-brown, Bowie launched his Ziggy Stardust stage show with <a href="/wiki/The_Spiders_from_Mars" title="The Spiders from Mars">the Spiders from Mars</a>—Ronson, Bolder, and Woodmansey—at the Toby Jug pub in <a href="/wiki/Tolworth" title="Tolworth">Tolworth</a> on 10 February 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-buckley135-6_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckley135-6-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The show was hugely popular, catapulting him to stardom as he toured the UK over the next six months and creating, as described by Buckley, a "cult of Bowie" that was "unique—its influence lasted longer and has been more creative than perhaps almost any other force within pop fandom."<sup id="cite_ref-buckley135-6_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckley135-6-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_Ziggy_Stardust_and_the_Spiders_from_Mars" title="The Rise and Fall of Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars">The Rise and Fall of Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars</a></i> (1972), combining the hard rock elements of <i>The Man Who Sold the World</i> with the lighter experimental rock and pop of <i>Hunky Dory</i>, was released in June. "<a href="/wiki/Starman_(song)" title="Starman (song)">Starman</a>", issued as an April single ahead of the album, was to cement Bowie's UK breakthrough: both single and album charted rapidly following his July <i><a href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops" title="Top of the Pops">Top of the Pops</a></i> performance of the song. The album, which remained in the chart for two years, was soon joined there by the 6-month-old <i>Hunky Dory</i>. At the same time the non-album single "<a href="/wiki/John,_I%27m_Only_Dancing" title="John, I&#39;m Only Dancing">John, I'm Only Dancing</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/All_the_Young_Dudes" title="All the Young Dudes">All the Young Dudes</a>", a song he wrote and produced for <a href="/wiki/Mott_the_Hoople" title="Mott the Hoople">Mott the Hoople</a>, were successful in the UK. The <a href="/wiki/Ziggy_Stardust_Tour" title="Ziggy Stardust Tour">Ziggy Stardust Tour</a> continued to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bowie contributed backing vocals, keyboards, and guitar to Reed's 1972 solo breakthrough <i><a href="/wiki/Transformer_(album)" title="Transformer (album)">Transformer</a></i>, co-producing the album with Mick Ronson.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The following year, Bowie co-produced and <a href="/wiki/Audio_mixing_(recorded_music)" title="Audio mixing (recorded music)">mixed</a> <a href="/wiki/The_Stooges" title="The Stooges">The Stooges</a> album <i><a href="/wiki/Raw_Power" title="Raw Power">Raw Power</a></i> alongside Iggy Pop.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> His own <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_Sane" title="Aladdin Sane">Aladdin Sane</a></i> (1973) topped the UK chart, his first number-one album. Described by Bowie as "Ziggy goes to America", it contained songs he wrote while travelling to and across the US during the earlier part of the Ziggy tour, which now continued to Japan to promote the new album. <i>Aladdin Sane</i> spawned the UK top five singles "<a href="/wiki/The_Jean_Genie" title="The Jean Genie">The Jean Genie</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Drive-In_Saturday" title="Drive-In Saturday">Drive-In Saturday</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bowie's love of acting led his total immersion in the characters he created for his music. "Offstage I'm a robot. Onstage I achieve emotion. It's probably why I prefer dressing up as Ziggy to being David." With satisfaction came severe personal difficulties: acting the same role over an extended period, it became impossible for him to separate Ziggy Stardust—and, later, the Thin White Duke—from his own character offstage. Ziggy, Bowie said, "wouldn't leave me alone for years. That was when it all started to go sour&#160;... My whole personality was affected. It became very dangerous. I really did have doubts about my sanity."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> His later Ziggy shows, which included songs from both <i>Ziggy Stardust</i> and <i>Aladdin Sane</i>, were ultra-theatrical affairs filled with shocking stage moments, such as Bowie stripping down to a <a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">sumo</a> wrestling loincloth or simulating <a href="/wiki/Oral_sex" title="Oral sex">oral sex</a> with Ronson's guitar.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowie:_An_Illustrated_Record_p.7_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowie:_An_Illustrated_Record_p.7-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Bowie toured and gave press conferences as Ziggy before a dramatic and abrupt on-stage "retirement" at London's <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith_Odeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hammersmith Odeon">Hammersmith Odeon</a> on 3 July 1973. Footage from the final show was released the same year for the film <i><a href="/wiki/Ziggy_Stardust_and_the_Spiders_from_Mars_(film)" title="Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars (film)">Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_-_Ziggy_Stardust.ogg" title="File:David Bowie - Ziggy Stardust.ogg">"Ziggy Stardust"</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="19.005532879819" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="David_Bowie_-_Ziggy_Stardust.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/51/David_Bowie_-_Ziggy_Stardust.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (68 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="68353" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/5/51/David_Bowie_-_Ziggy_Stardust.ogg/David_Bowie_-_Ziggy_Stardust.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="208616" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of "<a href="/wiki/Ziggy_Stardust_(song)" title="Ziggy Stardust (song)">Ziggy Stardust</a>" (1972). A pioneer of <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">glam rock</a>, Bowie performed as the character Ziggy Stardust, backed by <a href="/wiki/The_Spiders_from_Mars" title="The Spiders from Mars">the Spiders from Mars</a>.</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>After breaking up the Spiders from Mars, Bowie attempted to move on from his Ziggy persona. His back catalogue was now highly sought after: <i>The Man Who Sold the World</i> had been re-released in 1972 along with <i>Space Oddity</i>. "<a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars%3F" class="mw-redirect" title="Life on Mars?">Life on Mars?</a>", from <i>Hunky Dory</i>, was released in June 1973 and peaked at No. 3 on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">UK Singles Chart</a>. Entering the same chart in September, Bowie's novelty record from 1967, "<a href="/wiki/The_Laughing_Gnome" title="The Laughing Gnome">The Laughing Gnome</a>", reached No. 6.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Pin_Ups" title="Pin Ups">Pin Ups</a></i>, a collection of covers of his 1960s favourites, followed in October, producing a UK No. 3 hit in his version of <a href="/wiki/The_McCoys" title="The McCoys">the McCoys</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Sorrow_(The_McCoys_song)" title="Sorrow (The McCoys song)">Sorrow</a>" and itself peaking at number one, making David Bowie the best-selling act of 1973 in the UK. It brought the total number of Bowie albums concurrently on the UK chart to six.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1974.E2.80.931976:_.22Plastic_soul.22_and_the_Thin_White_Duke"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1974–1976:_&quot;Plastic_soul&quot;_and_the_Thin_White_Duke">1974–1976: "Plastic soul" and the Thin White Duke</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1974–1976: &quot;Plastic soul&quot; and the Thin White Duke">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_-_TopPop_1974_08.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/David_Bowie_-_TopPop_1974_08.png/220px-David_Bowie_-_TopPop_1974_08.png" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/David_Bowie_-_TopPop_1974_08.png/330px-David_Bowie_-_TopPop_1974_08.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/David_Bowie_-_TopPop_1974_08.png 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="422" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_-_TopPop_1974_08.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie filming a video for "<a href="/wiki/Rebel_Rebel" title="Rebel Rebel">Rebel Rebel</a>" in 1974</div></div></div>
<p>Bowie moved to the US in 1974, initially staying in New York City before settling in Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Diamond_Dogs" title="Diamond Dogs">Diamond Dogs</a></i> (1974), parts of which found him heading towards <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a>, was the product of two distinct ideas: a musical based on a wild future in a post-<a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" title="Apocalypse">apocalyptic</a> city, and setting <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four">1984</a></i> to music.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The album went to number one in the UK, spawning the hits "<a href="/wiki/Rebel_Rebel" title="Rebel Rebel">Rebel Rebel</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Diamond_Dogs_(song)" title="Diamond Dogs (song)">Diamond Dogs</a>", and No. 5 in the US. To promote it, Bowie launched the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Dogs_Tour" title="Diamond Dogs Tour">Diamond Dogs Tour</a>, visiting cities in North America between June and December 1974. Choreographed by <a href="/wiki/Toni_Basil" title="Toni Basil">Toni Basil</a>, and lavishly produced with theatrical special effects, the high-budget stage production was filmed by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Yentob" title="Alan Yentob">Alan Yentob</a>. The resulting documentary, <i><a href="/wiki/Cracked_Actor" title="Cracked Actor">Cracked Actor</a></i>, featured a pasty and emaciated Bowie: the tour coincided with the singer's slide from heavy <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> use into addiction, producing severe physical debilitation, <a href="/wiki/Paranoia" title="Paranoia">paranoia</a>, and emotional problems.<sup id="cite_ref-buckley_204-5_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckley_204-5-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> He later commented that the accompanying live album, <i><a href="/wiki/David_Live" title="David Live">David Live</a></i>, ought to have been titled "David Bowie Is Alive and Well and Living Only in Theory". <i>David Live</i> nevertheless solidified Bowie's status as a superstar, charting at No. 2 in the UK and No. 8 in the US. It also spawned a UK No. 10 hit in Bowie's cover of <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Floyd" title="Eddie Floyd">Eddie Floyd</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Knock_on_Wood_(song)" title="Knock on Wood (song)">Knock on Wood</a>". After a break in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, where Bowie recorded new material, the tour resumed with a new emphasis on soul.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bowie-DD-1974-3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bowie-DD-1974-3.jpg/170px-Bowie-DD-1974-3.jpg" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bowie-DD-1974-3.jpg/255px-Bowie-DD-1974-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/Bowie-DD-1974-3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="324" data-file-height="428" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bowie-DD-1974-3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie performing during <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Dogs_Tour" title="Diamond Dogs Tour">Diamond Dogs Tour</a>, 1974</div></div></div>
<p>The fruit of the Philadelphia recording sessions was <i><a href="/wiki/Young_Americans_(album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Young Americans (album)">Young Americans</a></i> (1975). Biographer Christopher Sandford writes, "Over the years, most British rockers had tried, one way or another, to become black-by-extension. Few had succeeded as Bowie did now."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The album's sound, which the singer identified as "<a href="/wiki/Plastic_soul" title="Plastic soul">plastic soul</a>", constituted a radical shift in style that initially alienated many of his UK devotees.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <i>Young Americans</i> yielded Bowie's first US number one, "<a href="/wiki/Fame_(David_Bowie_song)" title="Fame (David Bowie song)">Fame</a>", co-written with <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>, who contributed backing vocals, and <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Alomar" title="Carlos Alomar">Carlos Alomar</a>. Lennon called Bowie's work "great, but it's just rock'n'roll with lipstick on".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Earning the distinction of being one of the first white artists to appear on the US variety show <i><a href="/wiki/Soul_Train" title="Soul Train">Soul Train</a></i>, Bowie mimed "Fame", as well as "<a href="/wiki/Golden_Years_(song)" title="Golden Years (song)">Golden Years</a>", his November single,<sup id="cite_ref-sandfordoctobersingle_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandfordoctobersingle-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> which was originally offered to <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>, who declined it.<sup id="cite_ref-sandfordoctobersingle_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandfordoctobersingle-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> <i>Young Americans</i> was a commercial success in both the US and the UK, and a re-issue of the 1969 single "Space Oddity" became Bowie's first number-one hit in the UK a few months after "Fame" achieved the same in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Despite his by now well established superstardom, Bowie, in the words of Sandford, "for all his record sales (over a million copies of <i>Ziggy Stardust</i> alone), existed essentially on loose change."<sup id="cite_ref-firesDefries_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firesDefries-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In 1975, in a move echoing Ken Pitt's acrimonious dismissal five years earlier, Bowie fired his manager. At the culmination of the ensuing months-long legal dispute, he watched, as described by Sandford, "millions of dollars of his future earnings being surrendered" in what were "uniquely generous terms for Defries", then "shut himself up in West 20th Street, where for a week his howls could be heard through the locked attic door."<sup id="cite_ref-firesDefries_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firesDefries-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Michael Lippman, Bowie's lawyer during the negotiations, became his new manager; Lippman in turn was awarded substantial compensation when Bowie fired him the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG/220px-David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG/330px-David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG/440px-David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG 2x" data-file-width="840" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_and_Cher_1975.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie performing with <a href="/wiki/Cher" title="Cher">Cher</a> on the variety show <i><a href="/wiki/Cher_(TV_series)" title="Cher (TV series)">Cher</a></i>, 1975</div></div></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Station_to_Station" title="Station to Station">Station to Station</a></i> (1976), produced by Bowie and Harry Maslin,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>  introduced a new Bowie persona, "<a href="/wiki/The_Thin_White_Duke" title="The Thin White Duke">The Thin White Duke</a>" of its title-track. Visually, the character was an extension of Thomas Jerome Newton, the extraterrestrial being he portrayed in the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Fell_to_Earth" title="The Man Who Fell to Earth">The Man Who Fell to Earth</a></i> the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Developing the funk and soul of <i>Young Americans</i>, <i>Station to Station'</i>s synthesizer-heavy arrangements prefigured the <a href="/wiki/Krautrock" title="Krautrock">krautrock</a>-influenced music of his next releases. The extent to which drug addiction was now affecting Bowie was made public when <a href="/wiki/Russell_Harty" title="Russell Harty">Russell Harty</a> interviewed the singer for his <a href="/wiki/London_Weekend_Television" title="London Weekend Television">London Weekend Television</a> talk show in anticipation of the album's supporting tour. Shortly before the satellite-linked interview was scheduled to commence, the death of the Spanish dictator <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> was announced. Bowie was asked to relinquish the satellite booking, to allow the Spanish Government to put out a live newsfeed. This he refused to do, and his interview went ahead. In the ensuing lengthy conversation with Harty, Bowie was incoherent and looked "disconnected".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> His sanity—by his own later admission—had become twisted from cocaine; he overdosed several times during the year, and was withering physically to an alarming degree.<sup id="cite_ref-buckley_204-5_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckley_204-5-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sandford158_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford158-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_logo_(Diamond_Dogs).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/David_Bowie_logo_%28Diamond_Dogs%29.svg/220px-David_Bowie_logo_%28Diamond_Dogs%29.svg.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/David_Bowie_logo_%28Diamond_Dogs%29.svg/330px-David_Bowie_logo_%28Diamond_Dogs%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/David_Bowie_logo_%28Diamond_Dogs%29.svg/440px-David_Bowie_logo_%28Diamond_Dogs%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="571" data-file-height="359" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_logo_(Diamond_Dogs).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This era saw the debut of Bowie's famous <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Dogs" title="Diamond Dogs">Diamond Dogs</a> logo, reused as a general logo for the musician</div></div></div>
<p><i>Station to Station</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s January 1976 release was followed in February by a 3½-month-long concert tour of Europe and North America. Featuring a starkly lit set, the <a href="/wiki/Isolar_%E2%80%93_1976_Tour" title="Isolar – 1976 Tour">Isolar – 1976 Tour</a> with its iconic colour newsprint <a href="/w/index.php?title=Isolar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Isolar (page does not exist)">Isolar</a> concert program, highlighted songs from the album, including the dramatic and lengthy <a href="/wiki/Station_to_Station_(song)" title="Station to Station (song)">title track</a>, the ballads "<a href="/wiki/Wild_Is_the_Wind_(song)" title="Wild Is the Wind (song)">Wild Is the Wind</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Word_on_a_Wing" title="Word on a Wing">Word on a Wing</a>", and the funkier "<a href="/wiki/TVC_15" title="TVC 15">TVC 15</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Stay_(David_Bowie_song)" title="Stay (David Bowie song)">Stay</a>". The core band that coalesced to record this album and tour—rhythm guitarist <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Alomar" title="Carlos Alomar">Carlos Alomar</a>, bassist <a href="/wiki/George_Murray_(musician)" title="George Murray (musician)">George Murray</a>, and drummer <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Davis" title="Dennis Davis">Dennis Davis</a>—continued as a stable unit for the remainder of the 1970s. The tour was highly successful but mired in political controversy. Bowie was quoted in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> as saying that "Britain could benefit from a Fascist leader", and was detained by customs on the Russian/Polish border for possessing <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazi</a> paraphernalia.<sup id="cite_ref-Buckley_pp.289–91_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley_pp.289–91-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_1976.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/David_Bowie_1976.jpg/220px-David_Bowie_1976.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/David_Bowie_1976.jpg/330px-David_Bowie_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/David_Bowie_1976.jpg/440px-David_Bowie_1976.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_1976.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie as the Thin White Duke at <a href="/wiki/Maple_Leaf_Gardens" title="Maple Leaf Gardens">Maple Leaf Gardens</a>, Toronto, 1976</div></div></div>
<p>Matters came to a head in London in May in what became known as the "<a href="/wiki/London_Victoria_station" title="London Victoria station">Victoria Station</a> incident". Arriving in an open-top <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes</a> <a href="/wiki/Convertible" title="Convertible">convertible</a>, Bowie waved to the crowd in a gesture that some alleged was a <a href="/wiki/Nazi_salute" title="Nazi salute">Nazi salute</a>, which was captured on camera and published in <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i>. Bowie said the photographer simply caught him in mid-wave.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> He later blamed his pro-fascism comments and his behaviour during the period on his addictions and the character of the Thin White Duke.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> "I was out of my mind, totally crazed. The main thing I was functioning on was mythology&#160;... that whole thing about Hitler and Rightism&#160;... I'd discovered <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford158_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford158-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> According to playwright Alan Franks, writing later in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>, "he was indeed 'deranged'. He had some very bad experiences with hard drugs."<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Bowie's cocaine addiction, which had motivated these controversies, had much to do with his time living in Los Angeles, a city which alienated him. Discussing his flirtations with fascism in a 1980 interview with <i>NME</i>, Bowie explained that Los Angeles was "where it had all happened. The fucking place should be wiped off the face of the Earth. To be anything to do with rock and roll and go and live in Los Angeles is, I think, just heading for disaster. It really is."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After recovering from addiction, Bowie apologized for these statements, and throughout the 1980s and '90s criticized racism in European politics and the American music industry.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Bowie's comments on fascism, as well as <a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a>'s alcohol-fueled denunciations of Pakistani immigrants in 1976, led to the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Rock_Against_Racism" title="Rock Against Racism">Rock Against Racism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1976.E2.80.931979:_Berlin_era"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1976–1979:_Berlin_era">1976–1979: Berlin era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1976–1979: Berlin era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Trilogy" title="Berlin Trilogy">Berlin Trilogy</a></div>
<p>Bowie moved to Switzerland in 1976, purchasing a chalet in the hills to the north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Geneva" title="Lake Geneva">Lake Geneva</a>. In the new environment, his cocaine use decreased and he found time for other pursuits outside his musical career.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> He devoted more time to his painting, and produced a number of <a href="/wiki/Post-modernist" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-modernist">post-modernist</a> pieces. When on tour, he took to sketching in a notebook, and photographing scenes for later reference. Visiting galleries in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%BCcke_Museum" title="Brücke Museum">Brücke Museum</a> in Berlin, Bowie became, in the words of biographer Christopher Sandford, "a prolific producer and collector of contemporary art.&#160;... Not only did he become a well-known patron of <a href="/wiki/Expressionist_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Expressionist art">expressionist art</a>: locked in Clos des Mésanges he began an intensive self-improvement course in classical music and literature, and started work on an autobiography."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg/220px-Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg/330px-Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg/440px-Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hauptstra%C3%9Fe_155_Berlin-Sch%C3%B6neberg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apartment building at 155, Hauptstraße, <a href="/wiki/Sch%C3%B6neberg" title="Schöneberg">Schöneberg</a>, Berlin, where Bowie lived from 1976 to 1978</div></div></div>
<p>Before the end of 1976, Bowie's interest in the burgeoning German music scene, as well as his drug addiction, prompted him to move to <a href="/wiki/West_Berlin" title="West Berlin">West Berlin</a> to clean up and revitalise his career. There he was often seen riding a bicycle between his apartment on Hauptstraße in <a href="/wiki/Sch%C3%B6neberg" title="Schöneberg">Schöneberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hansa_Tonstudio" title="Hansa Tonstudio">Hansa Tonstudio</a>, the recording studio he used, located on Köthener Straße in <a href="/wiki/Kreuzberg" title="Kreuzberg">Kreuzberg</a>, near the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruether_82–85_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruether_82–85-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> While working with <a href="/wiki/Brian_Eno" title="Brian Eno">Brian Eno</a> and sharing an apartment with Iggy Pop, he began to focus on minimalist, ambient music for the first of three albums, co-produced with <a href="/wiki/Tony_Visconti" title="Tony Visconti">Tony Visconti</a>, that became known as his <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Trilogy" title="Berlin Trilogy">Berlin Trilogy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> During the same period, Iggy Pop, with Bowie as a co-writer and musician, completed his solo album debut <i><a href="/wiki/The_Idiot_(album)" title="The Idiot (album)">The Idiot</a></i> and its follow-up <i><a href="/wiki/Lust_for_Life_(Iggy_Pop_album)" title="Lust for Life (Iggy Pop album)">Lust for Life</a></i>, touring the UK, Europe, and the US in March and April 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_bowie_05061978_01_150.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/ff/David_bowie_05061978_01_150.jpg" width="150" height="221" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="150" data-file-height="221" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_bowie_05061978_01_150.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie performing in <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a>, Norway, 1978</div></div></div>
<p>The album <i><a href="/wiki/Low_(David_Bowie_album)" title="Low (David Bowie album)">Low</a></i> (1977), partly influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Krautrock" title="Krautrock">Krautrock</a> sound of <a href="/wiki/Kraftwerk" title="Kraftwerk">Kraftwerk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neu!" title="Neu!">Neu!</a>, evinced a move away from narration in Bowie's songwriting to a more abstract musical form in which lyrics were sporadic and optional. Although he completed the album in November 1976, it took his unsettled record company another three months to release it.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruether_82–85_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruether_82–85-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> It received considerable negative criticism upon its release—a release which RCA, anxious to maintain the established commercial momentum, did not welcome, and which Bowie's former manager, Tony Defries, who still maintained a significant financial interest in the singer's affairs, tried to prevent. Despite these forebodings, <i>Low</i> yielded the UK No. 3 single "<a href="/wiki/Sound_and_Vision" title="Sound and Vision">Sound and Vision</a>", and its own performance surpassed that of <i>Station to Station</i> in the UK chart, where it reached No. 2. Leading contemporary composer <a href="/wiki/Philip_Glass" title="Philip Glass">Philip Glass</a> described <i>Low</i> as "a work of genius" in 1992, when he used it as the basis for his <i><a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._1_(Glass)" title="Symphony No. 1 (Glass)">Symphony No. 1 "Low"</a></i>; subsequently, Glass used Bowie's next album as the basis for his 1996 <i><a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._4_(Glass)" title="Symphony No. 4 (Glass)">Symphony No. 4 "Heroes"</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-low_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-low-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Glass has praised Bowie's gift for creating "fairly complex pieces of music, masquerading as simple pieces".<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>  Also in 1977, <a href="/wiki/London_Records" title="London Records">London</a> released <i>Starting Point</i>, a ten-song LP containing releases from Bowie's <a href="/wiki/Deram_Records" title="Deram Records">Deram</a> period (1966—67).<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_-_Heroes.ogg" title="File:David Bowie - Heroes.ogg">"Heroes"</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.988571428571" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="David_Bowie_-_Heroes.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/aa/David_Bowie_-_Heroes.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (62 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="61895" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/a/aa/David_Bowie_-_Heroes.ogg/David_Bowie_-_Heroes.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="195688" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of "<a href="/wiki/%22Heroes%22_(David_Bowie_song)" title="&quot;Heroes&quot; (David Bowie song)">Heroes</a>" (1977). One of the ambient rock songs to emerge from Bowie's <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Trilogy" title="Berlin Trilogy">Berlin Trilogy</a> era, "Heroes" gained lasting popularity.</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Echoing <i>Low</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s minimalist, instrumental approach, the second of the trilogy, <i><a href="/wiki/%22Heroes%22_(David_Bowie_album)" title="&quot;Heroes&quot; (David Bowie album)">"Heroes"</a></i> (1977), incorporated pop and rock to a greater extent, seeing Bowie joined by guitarist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fripp" title="Robert Fripp">Robert Fripp</a>. Like <i>Low</i>, <i>"Heroes"</i> evinced the <a href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">zeitgeist</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, symbolised by the divided city of Berlin.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Incorporating ambient sounds from a variety of sources including white noise generators, synthesisers and <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">koto</a>, the album was another hit, reaching No. 3 in the UK. Its <a href="/wiki/%22Heroes%22_(song)" class="mw-redirect" title="&quot;Heroes&quot; (song)">title-track</a>, though only reaching No. 24 in the UK singles chart, gained lasting popularity, and within months had been released in both German and French.<sup id="cite_ref-heroes_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heroes-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Towards the end of the year, Bowie performed the song for Marc Bolan's television show <i><a href="/wiki/Marc_(TV_series)" title="Marc (TV series)">Marc</a></i>, and again two days later for <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a>'s final <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> television Christmas special, when he joined Crosby in "<a href="/wiki/Peace_on_Earth/Little_Drummer_Boy" title="Peace on Earth/Little Drummer Boy">Peace on Earth/Little Drummer Boy</a>", a version of "<a href="/wiki/The_Little_Drummer_Boy" title="The Little Drummer Boy">The Little Drummer Boy</a>" with a new, <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">contrapuntal</a> verse. Five years later, the duet proved a worldwide seasonal hit, charting in the UK at No. 3 on Christmas Day, 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After completing <i>Low</i> and <i>"Heroes"</i>, Bowie spent much of 1978 on the <a href="/wiki/Isolar_II_%E2%80%93_The_1978_World_Tour" title="Isolar II – The 1978 World Tour">Isolar II world tour</a>, bringing the music of the first two Berlin Trilogy albums to almost a million people during 70 concerts in 12 countries. By now he had broken his drug addiction; biographer David Buckley writes that Isolar II was "Bowie's first tour for five years in which he had probably not anaesthetised himself with copious quantities of cocaine before taking the stage.&#160;... Without the oblivion that drugs had brought, he was now in a healthy enough mental condition to want to make friends."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Recordings from the tour made up the live album <i><a href="/wiki/Stage_(David_Bowie_album)" title="Stage (David Bowie album)">Stage</a></i>, released the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The final piece in what Bowie called his "<a href="/wiki/Triptych" title="Triptych">triptych</a>", <i><a href="/wiki/Lodger_(album)" title="Lodger (album)">Lodger</a></i> (1979), eschewed the minimalist, ambient nature of the other two, making a partial return to the drum- and guitar-based rock and pop of his pre-Berlin era. The result was a complex mixture of <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">new wave</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_music" title="World music">world music</a>, in places incorporating <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_dominant_scale" title="Phrygian dominant scale">Hijaz</a> <a href="/wiki/Musical_scale#Non-Western_scales" class="mw-redirect" title="Musical scale">non-Western scales</a>.
