Bowie was a pioneer of glam rock, according to music historians Schinder and Schwartz, who credited <a href="/wiki/Marc_Bolan" title="Marc Bolan">Marc Bolan</a> and Bowie with creating the genre.<sup id="cite_ref-Schinder_&amp;_Schwartz_2007:_p._482_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schinder_&amp;_Schwartz_2007:_p._482-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, he inspired the innovators of the <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> music movement.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> When punk musicians were "noisily reclaiming the three-minute pop song in a show of public defiance", biographer David Buckley wrote that "Bowie almost completely abandoned traditional rock instrumentation."<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Bowie's record company promoted his unique status in popular music with the slogan, "There's old wave, there's new wave, and there's David Bowie".<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Musicologist James Perone credited him with having "brought sophistication to rock music", and critical reviews frequently acknowledged the intellectual depth of his work and influence.<sup id="cite_ref-Schinder_&amp;_Schwartz_2007:_p._482_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schinder_&amp;_Schwartz_2007:_p._482-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Human League">Human League</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Martyn_Ware" title="Martyn Ware">Martyn Ware</a> remarked on the depth of his pervasive artistry that he had lived his life "as though he were an art installation."<sup id="cite_ref-20K_Tweets_a_Minute_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20K_Tweets_a_Minute-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As described by <a href="/wiki/John_Peel" title="John Peel">John Peel</a>, "The one distinguishing feature about early-70s progressive rock was that it didn't progress. Before Bowie came along, people didn't want too much change". Buckley called the era "bloated, self-important, leather-clad, self-satisfied"; then Bowie "subverted the whole notion of what it was to be a rock star".
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>After Bowie there has been no other pop icon of his stature, because the pop world that produces these rock gods doesn't exist any more.&#160;... The fierce partisanship of the cult of Bowie was also unique—its influence lasted longer and has been more creative than perhaps almost any other force within pop fandom.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Buckley called Bowie "both star and icon. The vast body of work he has produced&#160;... has created perhaps the biggest cult in popular culture.&#160;... His influence has been unique in popular culture—he has permeated and altered more lives than any comparable figure."<sup id="cite_ref-buckley_legacy_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckley_legacy-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Through continual reinvention, his influence broadened and extended.<sup id="cite_ref-Filicky_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Filicky-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Biographer Thomas Forget added, "Because he has succeeded in so many different styles of music, it is almost impossible to find a popular artist today that has not been influenced by David Bowie."<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> In 2000, Bowie was voted by other music stars as the "most influential artist of all time" in a poll by <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Petridis" title="Alexis Petridis">Alexis Petridis</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> wrote that Bowie was confirmed by 1980 to be "the most important and influential artist since <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neil_McCormick" title="Neil McCormick">Neil McCormick</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> stated that Bowie had "one of the supreme careers in popular music, art and culture of the 20th century" and "he was too inventive, too mercurial, too strange for all but his most devoted fans to keep up with".<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> The BBC's <a href="/wiki/Mark_Easton" title="Mark Easton">Mark Easton</a> argued that Bowie provided fuel for "the creative powerhouse that Britain has become" by challenging future generations "to aim high, to be ambitious and provocative, to take risks". Easton concluded that Bowie had "changed the way the world sees Britain. And the way Britain sees itself".<sup id="cite_ref-easton_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-easton-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Annie Zaleski of <i><a href="/wiki/Alternative_Press_(music_magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alternative Press (music magazine)">Alternative Press</a></i> wrote, "Every band or solo artist who's decided to rip up their playbook and start again owes a debt to Bowie".<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, he was dubbed "The Greatest Rock Star Ever" by <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous figures from the music industry whose careers Bowie had influenced paid tribute to him following his death; <a href="/wiki/Panegyric" title="Panegyric">panegyrics</a> on Twitter (tweets about him peaked at 20,000 a minute an hour after his death)<sup id="cite_ref-20K_Tweets_a_Minute_246-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20K_Tweets_a_Minute-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> also came from outside the entertainment industry and <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">pop culture</a>, such as those from the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican</a>, namely Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Gianfranco_Ravasi" title="Gianfranco Ravasi">Gianfranco Ravasi</a>, who quoted "Space Oddity", and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Foreign_Office" title="Federal Foreign Office">Federal Foreign Office</a>, which thanked Bowie for his part in the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of the Berlin Wall">fall of the Berlin Wall</a> and referenced "Heroes".<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 January 2017 the BBC broadcast the 90-minute documentary <i>David Bowie: The Last Five Years</i>, taking a detailed look at Bowie's last albums, <i>The Next Day</i> and <i>Blackstar</i>, and his play <i>Lazarus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> On 8 January 2017, which would have been Bowie's 70th birthday, a charity concert in his birthplace of Brixton was hosted by the actor <a href="/wiki/Gary_Oldman" title="Gary Oldman">Gary Oldman</a>, a close friend.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> A David Bowie walking tour through Brixton was also launched, and other events marking his birthday weekend included concerts in New York, Los Angeles, Sydney, and Tokyo.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 February 2018 the <a href="/wiki/Falcon_Heavy_Test_Flight" class="mw-redirect" title="Falcon Heavy Test Flight">maiden flight</a> of the <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> <a href="/wiki/Falcon_Heavy" title="Falcon Heavy">Falcon Heavy</a> rocket carried <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">Elon Musk</a>'s personal <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk%27s_Tesla_Roadster" title="Elon Musk&#39;s Tesla Roadster">Tesla Roadster</a> and a mannequin affectionately named <a href="/wiki/Starman_(song)" title="Starman (song)">Starman</a> into space. "<a href="/wiki/Space_Oddity" title="Space Oddity">Space Oddity</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars%3F" class="mw-redirect" title="Life on Mars?">Life on Mars?</a>" were looping on the car's sound system during the launch.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Musicianship">Musicianship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Musicianship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar,_HRC_Warsaw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar%2C_HRC_Warsaw.jpg/220px-David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar%2C_HRC_Warsaw.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar%2C_HRC_Warsaw.jpg/330px-David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar%2C_HRC_Warsaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar%2C_HRC_Warsaw.jpg/440px-David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar%2C_HRC_Warsaw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie%27s_Vox_Mark_VI_guitar,_HRC_Warsaw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie's <a href="/wiki/Vox_(musical_equipment)" title="Vox (musical equipment)">Vox</a> <a href="/wiki/Vox_Mark_III" title="Vox Mark III">Mark VI guitar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hard_Rock_Cafe" title="Hard Rock Cafe">Hard Rock Cafe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, Poland</div></div></div>
<p>From the time of his earliest recordings in the 1960s, Bowie employed a wide variety of musical styles. His early compositions and performances were strongly influenced by rock and rollers like <a href="/wiki/Little_Richard" title="Little Richard">Little Richard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>, and also the wider world of show business. He particularly strove to emulate the British musical theatre singer-songwriter and actor <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Newley" title="Anthony Newley">Anthony Newley</a>, whose vocal style he frequently adopted, and made prominent use of for his 1967 debut release, <i><a href="/wiki/David_Bowie_(1967_album)" title="David Bowie (1967 album)">David Bowie</a></i> (to the disgust of Newley himself, who destroyed the copy he received from Bowie's publisher).<sup id="cite_ref-albumdebut_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-albumdebut-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Bowie's <a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">music hall</a> fascination continued to surface sporadically alongside such diverse styles as hard rock and heavy metal, soul, psychedelic folk, and pop.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Musicologist James Perone observes Bowie's use of octave switches for different repetitions of the same melody, exemplified in his commercial breakthrough single, "<a href="/wiki/Space_Oddity" title="Space Oddity">Space Oddity</a>", and later in the song "<a href="/wiki/%22Heroes%22_(song)" class="mw-redirect" title="&quot;Heroes&quot; (song)">Heroes</a>", to dramatic effect; Perone notes that "in the lowest part of his vocal register&#160;... his voice has an almost crooner-like richness."<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Voice instructor Jo Thompson describes Bowie's vocal vibrato technique as "particularly deliberate and distinctive".<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> Schinder and Schwartz call him "a vocalist of extraordinary technical ability, able to pitch his singing to particular effect."<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Here, too, as in his stagecraft and songwriting, the singer's role playing is evident: historiographer Michael Campbell says that Bowie's lyrics "arrest our ear, without question. But Bowie continually shifts from person to person as he delivers them&#160;... His voice changes dramatically from section to section."<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> In a 2014 analysis of 77 "top" artists' vocal ranges, Bowie was 8th, just behind <a href="/wiki/Christina_Aguilera" title="Christina Aguilera">Christina Aguilera</a> and just ahead of <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the guitar, Bowie also played a variety of keyboards, including piano, Mellotron, Chamberlin, and synthesizers; harmonica; alto and baritone saxophones; <a href="/wiki/Stylophone" title="Stylophone">stylophone</a>; <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>; cello; <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">koto</a> (in the <i>Heroes</i> track "Moss Garden"); <a href="/wiki/Mbira" title="Mbira">thumb piano</a>; drums (on the <i>Heathen</i> track "Cactus"), and various percussion instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Painter_and_art_collector">Painter and art collector</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Painter and art collector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie%27s_art_collection" title="David Bowie&#39;s art collection">David Bowie's art collection</a></div>
<p>Bowie was also a painter and artist. One of his paintings sold at auction in late 1990 for $500,<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> and the cover for his 1995 album <i><a href="/wiki/Outside_(David_Bowie_album)" title="Outside (David Bowie album)">Outside</a></i> is a close-up of a self-portrait (from a series of five) he painted that same year.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> His first solo show was at The Gallery, <a href="/wiki/Cork_Street" title="Cork Street">Cork Street</a> in 1995, entitled 'New Afro/Pagan and Work: 1975–1995'.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> He was invited to join the editorial board of the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Painters_(magazine)" title="Modern Painters (magazine)">Modern Painters</a></i> in 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-TGS_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TGS-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> and participated in the <a href="/wiki/Nat_Tate:_An_American_Artist_1928%E2%80%931960" title="Nat Tate: An American Artist 1928–1960">Nat Tate</a> art hoax later that year.<sup id="cite_ref-NW98_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW98-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998 during an interview with <a href="/wiki/Michael_Kimmelman" title="Michael Kimmelman">Michael Kimmelman</a> for <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a> he said "Art was, seriously, the only thing I'd ever wanted to own.",<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> subsequently in 1999, in an interview for the BBC, he said "The only thing I buy obsessively and addictively is art".<sup id="cite_ref-ibt_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibt-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> His art collection, which included works by <a href="/wiki/Damien_Hirst" title="Damien Hirst">Damien Hirst</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Auerbach" title="Frank Auerbach">Frank Auerbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moore" title="Henry Moore">Henry Moore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Michel_Basquiat" title="Jean-Michel Basquiat">Jean-Michel Basquiat</a> among others, was valued at over £10m in mid-2016.<sup id="cite_ref-TGS_278-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TGS-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After his death his family decided to sell most of the collection because they "didn't have the space" to store it.<sup id="cite_ref-TGS_278-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TGS-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> On 10 and 11 November three auctions were held at <a href="/wiki/Sotheby%27s" title="Sotheby&#39;s">Sotheby's</a> in London, first with 47 lots and second with 208 paintings, drawings, and sculptures, third with 100 design lots.<sup id="cite_ref-sh_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sh-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> The items on sale represented about 65 percent of the collection.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> Exhibition of the works in the auction attracted 51,470 visitors, the auction itself was attended by 1,750 bidders, with over 1,000 more bidding online. The auctions has overall sale total £32.9&#160;million (app. $41.5&#160;million), while the highest-selling item, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Michel_Basquiat" title="Jean-Michel Basquiat">Jean-Michel Basquiat</a>'s graffiti-inspired painting <i>Air Power</i>, sold for £7.09&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-sh_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sh-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg/220px-Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg/330px-Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg/440px-Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2557" data-file-height="2036" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iman_and_David_Bowie_at_the_premiere_of_Moon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bowie and wife <a href="/wiki/Iman_(model)" title="Iman (model)">Iman</a>, 2009</div></div></div>
<p>Bowie married his first wife, <a href="/wiki/Angela_Bowie" class="mw-redirect" title="Angela Bowie">Mary Angela Barnett</a> on 19 March 1970 at Bromley Register Office in <a href="/wiki/Bromley" title="Bromley">Bromley</a>, London. Their son <a href="/wiki/Duncan_Jones" title="Duncan Jones">Duncan</a>, born on 30 May 1971, was at first known as Zowie.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> Bowie and Angela divorced on 8 February 1980 in Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 24 April 1992, Bowie married <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somali</a>-American model <a href="/wiki/Iman_(model)" title="Iman (model)">Iman</a> in a private ceremony in <a href="/wiki/Lausanne" title="Lausanne">Lausanne</a>. The wedding was later solemnised on 6 June in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pegg_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pegg-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> They had one daughter, Alexandria "Lexi" Zahra Jones, born in August 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Fltnib_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fltnib-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fmdiman_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fmdiman-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> The couple resided primarily in New York City and London, as well as owning an apartment in Sydney's <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Bay,_New_South_Wales" title="Elizabeth Bay, New South Wales">Elizabeth Bay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dumas_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dumas-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> and Britannia Bay House on the island of Mustique,<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> now renamed <a href="/wiki/Mandalay_Estate_Mustique" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandalay Estate Mustique">Mandalay Estate</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality">Sexuality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sexuality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bowie declared himself gay in an interview with Michael Watts for a 1972 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> coinciding with his campaign for stardom as Ziggy Stardust.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowie:_An_Illustrated_Record_p.7_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowie:_An_Illustrated_Record_p.7-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> According to Buckley, "If Ziggy confused both his creator and his audience, a big part of that confusion centred on the topic of sexuality."<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> In a September 1976 interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i>, Bowie said, "It's true—I am a <a href="/wiki/Bisexuality" title="Bisexuality">bisexual</a>. But I can't deny that I've used that fact very well. I suppose it's the best thing that ever happened to me."<sup id="cite_ref-playboy_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-playboy-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> His first wife, Angie, supports his claim of bisexuality and alleges that Bowie had a relationship with <a href="/wiki/Mick_Jagger" title="Mick Jagger">Mick Jagger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 1983 interview with <i>Rolling Stone</i>, Bowie said his public declaration of bisexuality was "the biggest mistake I ever made" and "I was always a closet heterosexual."<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> On other occasions, he said his interest in homosexual and bisexual culture had been more a product of the times and the situation in which he found himself than of his own feelings.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Blender_(magazine)" title="Blender (magazine)">Blender</a></i> asked Bowie in 2002 whether he still believed his public declaration was his biggest mistake. After a long pause, he said, "I don't think it was a mistake in Europe, but it was a lot tougher in America. I had no problem with people knowing I was bisexual. But I had no inclination to hold any banners nor be a representative of any group of people." Bowie said he wanted to be a songwriter and performer rather than a headline for his bisexuality, and in "<a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">puritanical</a>" America, "I think it stood in the way of so much I wanted to do."<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Buckley wrote that Bowie "mined sexual intrigue for its ability to shock",<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> and was probably "never gay, nor even consistently actively bisexual", instead experimenting "out of a sense of curiosity and a genuine allegiance with the 'transgressional'."<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Biographer Christopher Sandford said, according to Mary Finnigan—with whom Bowie had an affair in 1969—the singer and his first wife Angie "created their bisexual fantasy".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford48_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford48-305">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Sandford wrote that Bowie "made a positive fetish of repeating the quip that he and his wife had met while 'fucking the same bloke'&#160;... Gay sex was always an anecdotal and laughing matter. That Bowie's actual tastes swung the other way is clear from even a partial tally of his affairs with women."<sup id="cite_ref-sandford48_305-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford48-305">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> The BBC's <a href="/wiki/Mark_Easton" title="Mark Easton">Mark Easton</a> wrote in 2016 that Britain was "far more tolerant of difference" and that gay rights, such as same-sex marriage, and gender equality would not have "enjoyed the broad support they do today without Bowie's androgynous challenge all those years ago".<sup id="cite_ref-easton_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-easton-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spirituality_and_religion">Spirituality and religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Spirituality and religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Over the years, Bowie made numerous references to religions and to his evolving spirituality. Beginning in 1967, he showed an interest in <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>; after a few months' study at Tibet House in London, he was told by a <a href="/wiki/Lama" title="Lama">Lama</a>, "You don't want to be Buddhist.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... You should follow music."<sup id="cite_ref-newsday.com_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsday.com-306">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> By 1975, Bowie admitted, "I felt totally, absolutely alone. And I probably was alone because I pretty much had abandoned God."<sup id="cite_ref-Arena1993_300-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arena1993-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Bowie married Iman in a private ceremony in 1992, he said they knew that their "real marriage, sanctified by God, had to happen in a church in Florence".<sup id="cite_ref-PJ_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PJ-307">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> Earlier that year, he knelt on stage at <a href="/wiki/The_Freddie_Mercury_Tribute_Concert" title="The Freddie Mercury Tribute Concert">The Freddie Mercury Tribute Concert</a> and recited the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord&#39;s Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a> before a television audience.<sup id="cite_ref-LA_Times_Mercury_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LA_Times_Mercury-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, Bowie said he had an "undying" belief in the "unquestionable" existence of God.<sup id="cite_ref-Arena1993_300-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arena1993-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> In a separate 1993 interview, while describing the genesis of the music for his album <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Tie_White_Noise" title="Black Tie White Noise">Black Tie White Noise</a></i>, he said "..it was important for me to find something [musically] that also had no sort of representation of institutionalized and organized religion, of which I'm not a believer, I must make that clear."<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> Interviewed in 2005, Bowie said whether God exists "is not a question that can be answered.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... I'm not quite an atheist and it worries me. There's that little bit that holds on: 'Well, I'm <i>almost</i> an atheist. Give me a couple of months.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... I've nearly got it right.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-DeCurtis2005_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeCurtis2005-310">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> In his will, Bowie stipulated that he be <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremated</a> and his ashes scattered in <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a> "in accordance with the Buddhist rituals".<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"Questioning my spiritual life has always been germane" to Bowie's songwriting.<sup id="cite_ref-DeCurtis2005_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeCurtis2005-310">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Station_to_Station_(song)" title="Station to Station (song)">Station to Station</a>" is "very much concerned with the <a href="/wiki/Stations_of_the_Cross" title="Stations of the Cross">Stations of the Cross</a>"; the song also specifically references <a href="/wiki/Christian_Kabbalah" title="Christian Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>. Bowie called the album "extremely dark<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... the nearest album to a <a href="/wiki/Magick_(Thelema)" title="Magick (Thelema)">magick</a> treatise that I've written".<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Earthling_(album)" title="Earthling (album)">Earthling</a></i> showed "the abiding need in me to vacillate between <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a> or a kind of <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">gnosticism</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... What I need is to find a balance, spiritually, with the way I live and my demise."<sup id="cite_ref-Q97X_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Q97X-315">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> Released shortly before his death, "<a href="/wiki/Lazarus_(David_Bowie_song)" title="Lazarus (David Bowie song)">Lazarus</a>"—from his final album, <i><a href="/wiki/Blackstar_(album)" title="Blackstar (album)">Blackstar</a></i>—began with the words, "Look up here, I'm in Heaven" while the rest of the album deals with other matters of mysticism and mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1976, speaking as <a href="/wiki/The_Thin_White_Duke" title="The Thin White Duke">The Thin White Duke</a>, Bowie's persona at the time, and "at least partially tongue-in-cheek", he made statements that expressed support for fascism and perceived admiration for <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> in interviews with <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i>, <i>NME</i>, and a Swedish publication. Bowie was quoted as saying: "Britain is ready for a fascist leader... I think Britain could benefit from a fascist leader. After all, fascism is really nationalism... I believe very strongly in fascism, people have always responded with greater efficiency under a regimental leadership." He was also quoted as saying: "Adolf Hitler was one of the first rock stars" and "You've got to have an <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">extreme right</a> front come up and sweep everything off its feet and tidy everything up."<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> Bowie later retracted these comments in an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i> in October 1977, blaming them on mental instability caused by his drug problems at the time, saying: "I was out of my mind, totally, completely crazed."<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1980s and 1990s, Bowie's public statements shifted sharply towards <a href="/wiki/Anti-racism" title="Anti-racism">anti-racism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-fascism" title="Anti-fascism">anti-fascism</a>. In an interview with <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a> in 1983, Bowie criticised the channel for not providing enough coverage of black musicians,<sup id="cite_ref-MTV_video_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MTV_video-320">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jet_Black_artists_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jet_Black_artists-321">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> and the music videos for "China Girl" and "Let's Dance" were described by Bowie as a "very simple, very direct" statement against racism.<sup id="cite_ref-RS83_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RS83-322">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> The album <i>Tin Machine</i> took a more direct stance against fascism and Neo-Nazism, and was criticised for being too preachy.<sup id="cite_ref-tinmachine1_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tinmachine1-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, filmmaker and activist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moore" title="Michael Moore">Michael Moore</a> said he had wanted to use "<a href="/wiki/Panic_in_Detroit" title="Panic in Detroit">Panic in Detroit</a>" for his 1998 documentary <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_One_(film)" title="The Big One (film)">The Big One</a></i>; denied at first, he was given the rights after calling Bowie personally. "I've read stuff since his death saying that he wasn't that political and he stayed away from politics. But that wasn't the conversation that I had with him."<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_of_David_Bowie" title="Death of David Bowie">Death of David Bowie</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG/220px-David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG/330px-David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG/440px-David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_Death_New_York_Apartment_Memorial_2016_7.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woman places flowers outside Bowie's apartment in New York on <a href="/wiki/Lafayette_Street" title="Lafayette Street">Lafayette Street</a> the day after his death was announced</div></div></div>
<p>On 10 January 2016, two days after his 69th birthday and the release of the album <i><a href="/wiki/Blackstar_(album)" title="Blackstar (album)">Blackstar</a></i>, Bowie died from <a href="/wiki/Liver_cancer" title="Liver cancer">liver cancer</a> in his New York City apartment.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> He had been diagnosed 18 months earlier but had not made the news of his illness public.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> The Belgian theatre director <a href="/wiki/Ivo_van_Hove" title="Ivo van Hove">Ivo van Hove</a>, who had worked with the singer on his <a href="/wiki/Off-Broadway" title="Off-Broadway">Off-Broadway</a> musical <i><a href="/wiki/Lazarus_(musical)" title="Lazarus (musical)">Lazarus</a></i>, explained that Bowie was unable to attend rehearsals due to the progression of the disease. He noted that Bowie had kept working during the illness.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bowie's producer Tony Visconti wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He always did what he wanted to do. And he wanted to do it his way and he wanted to do it the best way. His death was no different from his life – a work of art. He made <i>Blackstar</i> for us, his parting gift. I knew for a year this was the way it would be. I wasn't, however, prepared for it. He was an extraordinary man, full of love and life. He will always be with us. For now, it is appropriate to cry.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Following Bowie's death, fans gathered at impromptu street shrines.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> At the mural of Bowie in his birthplace of Brixton, south London, which shows him in his <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_Sane" title="Aladdin Sane">Aladdin Sane</a></i> character, fans laid flowers and sang his songs.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> Other memorial sites included Berlin, Los Angeles, and outside his apartment in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> After news of his death, sales of his albums and singles soared.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> Bowie had insisted that he did not want a funeral, and according to his death certificate he was cremated in New Jersey on 12 January.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> As he wished in his will, his ashes were scattered in <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>, Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-ashes_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashes-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_recognition">Awards and recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Awards and recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_nominations_received_by_David_Bowie" title="List of awards and nominations received by David Bowie">List of awards and nominations received by David Bowie</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_(2014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_%282014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit%29.jpg/220px-David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_%282014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_%282014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit%29.jpg/330px-David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_%282014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_%282014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit%29.jpg/440px-David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_%282014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie%27s_Outfits_-_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame_(2014-12-30_13.09.55_by_Sam_Howzit).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Variety of Bowie's outfits on display at the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a></div></div></div>
<p>Bowie's 1969 commercial breakthrough, the song "Space Oddity", won him an <a href="/wiki/Ivor_Novello_Awards" title="Ivor Novello Awards">Ivor Novello</a> Special Award For Originality.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> For his performance in the 1976 science fiction film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Fell_to_Earth" title="The Man Who Fell to Earth">The Man Who Fell to Earth</a></i>, he won a <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Saturn Award for Best Actor">Saturn Award for Best Actor</a>. In the ensuing decades he was honoured with numerous awards for his music and its accompanying videos, receiving, among others, six <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Awards</a><sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59thGA_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59thGA-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> and four <a href="/wiki/Brit_Awards" title="Brit Awards">Brit Awards</a>—winning Best British Male Artist twice; the award for Outstanding Contribution to Music in 1996; and the Brits Icon award for his "lasting impact on <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Culture of the United Kingdom">British culture</a>", given posthumously in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Icon_2016_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Icon_2016-337">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999, Bowie was made a Commander of the <a href="/wiki/Ordre_des_Arts_et_des_Lettres" title="Ordre des Arts et des Lettres">Ordre des Arts et des Lettres</a> by the French government.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> He received an honorary doctorate from <a href="/wiki/Berklee_College_of_Music" title="Berklee College of Music">Berklee College of Music</a> the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup> He declined the royal honour of <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_British_Empire" title="Order of the British Empire">Commander of the Order of the British Empire</a> (CBE) in 2000, and turned down a <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knighthood</a> in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> Bowie later stated "I would never have any intention of accepting anything like that. I seriously don't know what it's for. It's not what I spent my life working for."<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg/220px-David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg/330px-David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg/440px-David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Bowie_Exhibit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Bowie exhibit at <a href="/wiki/Broadway%E2%80%93Lafayette_Street/Bleecker_Street_(New_York_City_Subway)" title="Broadway–Lafayette Street/Bleecker Street (New York City Subway)">Broadway-Lafayette St.</a> Station</div></div></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Telegraph</a></i> in 2016 estimated Bowie's total worldwide sales at 140 million records.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, he was awarded 9 platinum, 11 gold, and 8 silver albums, and in the United States, 5 platinum and 9 gold.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2003, six of Bowie's albums appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s list of the <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone%27s_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="Rolling Stone&#39;s 500 Greatest Albums of All Time">500 Greatest Albums of All Time</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> In 2004, four of Bowie's songs appeared on the <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> list of the <a href="/wiki/500_Greatest_Songs_of_All_Time" class="mw-redirect" title="500 Greatest Songs of All Time">500 Greatest Songs of All Time</a>. Additionally, four of Bowie's songs are included in <a href="/wiki/The_Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame%27s_500_Songs_that_Shaped_Rock_and_Roll" class="mw-redirect" title="The Rock and Roll Hall of Fame&#39;s 500 Songs that Shaped Rock and Roll">The Rock and Roll Hall of Fame's 500 Songs that Shaped Rock and Roll</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David.bowie.mural.sarajevo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/David.bowie.mural.sarajevo.jpg/220px-David.bowie.mural.sarajevo.jpg" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/David.bowie.mural.sarajevo.jpg/330px-David.bowie.mural.sarajevo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/8e/David.bowie.mural.sarajevo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="421" data-file-height="236" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David.bowie.mural.sarajevo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mural of Bowie in different guises in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the BBC's 2002 poll of the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a>, he was ranked 29.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> In 2004, <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine ranked him 39th on their list of the 100 Greatest Rock Artists of All Time.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> Bowie was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> on 17 January 1996<sup id="cite_ref-rockhall_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rockhall-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> and named a member of the <a href="/wiki/Science_Fiction_and_Fantasy_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="Science Fiction and Fantasy Hall of Fame">Science Fiction and Fantasy Hall of Fame</a> in June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-sffhof2013_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sffhof2013-352">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, <i>Rolling Stone</i> proclaimed Bowie "the greatest rock star ever".<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, the spider <i><a href="/wiki/Heteropoda_davidbowie" title="Heteropoda davidbowie">Heteropoda davidbowie</a></i> was named in Bowie's honour.<sup id="cite_ref-ARKive_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARKive-355">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> On 5 January 2015, a main-belt <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> was named <a href="/wiki/342843_Davidbowie" title="342843 Davidbowie">342843 Davidbowie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> On 13 January 2016, Belgian amateur astronomers at MIRA Public Observatory created a "Bowie <a href="/wiki/Asterism_(astronomy)" title="Asterism (astronomy)">asterism</a>" of seven stars which had been in the vicinity of Mars at the time of Bowie's death; the "constellation" forms the lightning bolt on Bowie's face from the cover of his <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_Sane" title="Aladdin Sane">Aladdin Sane</a></i> album.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 25 March 2018 a statue of Bowie was unveiled in <a href="/wiki/Aylesbury" title="Aylesbury">Aylesbury</a>, Buckinghamshire, the town where he debuted Ziggy Stardust.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> The statue features a likeness of Bowie in 2002 accompanied with his alter egos, with Ziggy at the front.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discography">Discography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Discography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie_discography" title="David Bowie discography">David Bowie discography</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Studio_albums">Studio albums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Studio albums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Filmography">Filmography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Filmography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie_filmography" title="David Bowie filmography">David Bowie filmography</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_film_roles">Selected film roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Selected film roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who_Fell_to_Earth" title="The Man Who Fell to Earth">The Man Who Fell to Earth</a></i> (1976) as Thomas Jerome Newton; received a <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Award_for_Best_Actor" title="Saturn Award for Best Actor">Saturn Award for Best Actor</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Just_a_Gigolo_(1978_film)" title="Just a Gigolo (1978 film)">Just a Gigolo</a></i> (1978) as Paul Ambrosius von Przygodski</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Christiane_F._%E2%80%93_Wir_Kinder_vom_Bahnhof_Zoo_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Christiane F. – Wir Kinder vom Bahnhof Zoo (film)">Christiane F.</a></i> (1981) cameo as himself</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Snowman" title="The Snowman">The Snowman</a></i> (1982) narrator in re-released version</li>
<li><i>Baal</i> (1982) as Baal</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Yellowbeard" title="Yellowbeard">Yellowbeard</a></i> (1983) the sailor wearing shark fin – The Shark</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Merry_Christmas,_Mr._Lawrence" title="Merry Christmas, Mr. Lawrence">Merry Christmas, Mr. Lawrence</a></i> (1983) as Maj. Jack 'Strafer' Celliers</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Hunger_(1983_film)" title="The Hunger (1983 film)">The Hunger</a></i> (1983) as John Blaylock</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Jazzin%27_for_Blue_Jean" title="Jazzin&#39; for Blue Jean">Jazzin' for Blue Jean</a></i> (1984) as Vic and Screaming Lord Byron</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Into_the_Night_(film)" title="Into the Night (film)">Into the Night</a></i> (1985) as Colin Morris</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Labyrinth_(film)" title="Labyrinth (film)">Labyrinth</a></i> (1986) as <a href="/wiki/Jareth" title="Jareth">Jareth the Goblin King</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Absolute_Beginners_(film)" title="Absolute Beginners (film)">Absolute Beginners</a></i> (1986) as Vendice Partners</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Temptation_of_Christ_(film)" title="The Last Temptation of Christ (film)">The Last Temptation of Christ</a></i> (1988) as <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Linguini_Incident" title="The Linguini Incident">The Linguini Incident</a></i> (1991) as Monte</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Twin_Peaks:_Fire_Walk_with_Me" title="Twin Peaks: Fire Walk with Me">Twin Peaks: Fire Walk with Me</a></i> (1992) as <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Jeffries" title="Phillip Jeffries">Phillip Jeffries</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dream_On_(TV_series)" title="Dream On (TV series)">Dream On</a></i> (television series) (1991) as Sir Roland Moorecock</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Basquiat_(film)" title="Basquiat (film)">Basquiat</a></i> (1996) as <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Gunslinger%27s_Revenge" title="Gunslinger&#39;s Revenge">Gunslinger's Revenge</a></i> (1998) as Jack Sikora</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Everybody_Loves_Sunshine" title="Everybody Loves Sunshine">Everybody Loves Sunshine</a></i> (1999) as Bernie</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Rice%27s_Secret" title="Mr. Rice&#39;s Secret">Mr. Rice's Secret</a></i> (2000) as William Rice</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Zoolander" title="Zoolander">Zoolander</a></i> (2001) cameo as himself; nominated for a <a href="/wiki/MTV_Movie_Award_for_Best_Cameo" title="MTV Movie Award for Best Cameo">MTV Movie Award for Best Cameo</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Arthur_and_the_Invisibles" title="Arthur and the Invisibles">Arthur and the Invisibles</a></i> (2006) as Malthazar (voice)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Prestige_(film)" title="The Prestige (film)">The Prestige</a></i> (2006) as <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bandslam" title="Bandslam">Bandslam</a> </i> (2009) – brief cameo role</li></ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Bowie&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-301"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-301">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1993, Bowie recalled having read <i><a href="/wiki/City_of_Night" title="City of Night">City of Night</a></i> in the 1960s, and it connected with his loneliness. "And that led me a merry dance in the early Seventies, when gay clubs really became my lifestyle and all my friends were gay".<sup id="cite_ref-Arena1993_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arena1993-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-308"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-308">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Asked why he knelt and prayed, Bowie said he had a friend who was dying of AIDS. "He was just dropping into a coma that day. And just before I went on stage something just told me to say the Lord's Prayer. The great irony is that he died two days after the show".<sup id="cite_ref-Arena1993_300-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arena1993-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-314"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-314">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He later said he was influenced by his cocaine addiction and the "psychological terror" from making <i>The Man Who Fell To Earth</i>, marking "the first time I'd really seriously thought about Christ and God&#160;... I very nearly got suckered into that narrow [view of] finding the Cross as the salvation of mankind".<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-346"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-346">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Additional sources place this figure somewhere between 100 million and 150 million.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
